<div id="review_1032577810" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1032577810" />
    <a title="Mel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5866692-mel"><img alt="Mel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359228708p2/5866692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1032577810?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mel" href="/user/show/5866692-mel">Mel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=chinese-literature">chinese-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=ming-dynasty">ming-dynasty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=period-of-disunion">period-of-disunion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1032577810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1266783686803803785">I started reading "The Three Kingdoms" a few weeks ago. I finished volume 1 this weekend. I'm reading the Moss Roberts translation as my Chinese is nowhere near good enough to tackle reading it in the original. I have to say I'm enjoying it much more than I thought I would. I have to say I think having played Dynasty Warriors 4 first really helped. With the cast of 100's having played the video game and been able to put faces to people helped keep them all straight a lot easier. I also think hav</span>
  <span id="freeText1266783686803803785" style="display:none">I started reading "The Three Kingdoms" a few weeks ago. I finished volume 1 this weekend. I'm reading the Moss Roberts translation as my Chinese is nowhere near good enough to tackle reading it in the original. I have to say I'm enjoying it much more than I thought I would. I have to say I think having played Dynasty Warriors 4 first really helped. With the cast of 100's having played the video game and been able to put faces to people helped keep them all straight a lot easier. I also think having studied the Han and knowing the history makes it more interesting too. One of my favorite things about the Han was the way they took children and folk songs as omens of events that were happening. When these songs were popular people would interpret the meaning and it's reflection on the dynasty, part of the whole mandate of heaven. I'm so pleased to see this incorporated into the book. When events are happening advisers will say have you heard this song popular in (wherever) I think it means this and you should therefore do this. <br /><br />I found that I quite liked the character of LuBu and was sorry to see him go. I felt he got a bad reputation as really he wasn't that much worse than Liu Bei swapping allegiances all the time. I thought there were some good moments, the one that sticks out the most was when Liu Bei, the hero, was fleeing for his life and having people feed him and protect him, he stopped at a hunters house, but the hunter could find no game to kill for him. So the hunter killed his own wife and fed her to Liu Bei, when Liu Bei asked what the meat was the hunter told him it was wolf. The next morning Liu Bei found her body, minus the arms, and realised what had happened. He was not horrified as his cannibalism but in awe of the great loyalty of the man and recommended him for honors when he returned to safety for the man's great sacrifice. <br /><br />I also like how there is no equivalent of "don't kill the messenger" whenever anyone delivers a message that they don't like the messenger is always killed! But so far it's not just been lots of battles, though there have been a fair few. There's also a lot of politics and intrigues and plots and that's been very enjoyable. Loyalties shift and switch a great deal, and there are a lot of executions. But I enjoyed the portrayal of the problems of power between the eunuchs and the families of the Imperial consorts. While not having the characterization of "A Dream of Red Mansions" there is a lot more than I was expecting. I think one of my favorite moments of Cao Cao was when he accidentally killed the family that was protecting him because he thought he was going to be killed, but they were just talking about a pig. After wards when he saw his friend on the road he had to kill him anyway, even though his friend was loyal, as otherwise the whole village would have pursued him for what he'd done. Such is the making of the villain.<br /><br />This morning I finished reading Three Kingdoms Volume 2. I'm still enjoying it very much. Volume 2 had Zhuge Liang appear and that was really great. Volume 2 had the famous battle at Red Cliff where they set Cao Cao's boats on fire. The local international channel here is showing the tv version of Three Kingdoms, (in Mandarin with traditional subtitles) I was finally able to pass where they were at in the story and suddenly it became much easier to watch when I knew what was going already. I enjoyed reading about Zhuge Liang's strategies a great deal. He does seem to be a bit too perfect, but that's also kind of fun. I also like the version I'm reading a lot as when discussing him calling the wind in the battle at red cliffs the footnote stated," On Dun Jia (evading stems), the technique by which Kongming will summon the winds see Kenneth J Dewoskin Doctors, Diviners and Magicians Of Ancient China" I think I shall have to look that up!<br /><br />But the battle of red cliffs wasn't the only fun part, there was also Liu Bei's wedding to Lady Sun which was really great. I like her as she also studied the Martial Arts and had all her maids taught and when Liu Bei showed up in her room for the wedding night it was filled with weapons.<br /><br />It was also interesting to see how ruthless the advisers were, particularly Pang Tong, despite working for the "hero" he was full of advice about assassinating people and stealing their countries while you could. Even when Liu Bei absolutely refused he was still hatching plots to get people killed. It was interesting to see how ruthless everyone really was. <br /><br />I'm glad I'm still enjoying the book, now I'm half way through (at 1100 pages) I think I might take a quick break to read the Ming dynasty stories that I bought. Though part of me is worried if I do take the break I'll never be able to remember who everyone is when I go back. Still I guess that's what the info screen on the video game is there for.<br /><br />Last night I finished reading Three Kingdoms Vol.3. I enjoyed the beginning they had a Taoist magician show up, and a really neat fortune teller. It was interesting to read the descriptions about what they could do. In fact I think I have come across the description of those two in some of the academic books I've been reading. After that it got a bit repetitious and then all of a sudden everyone started dying! Which made it interesting and fun again. Half way through the book the three main hero characters died, as did the villain, and sons and advisers took over. I liked the fact that Zhang Fei was murdered in his sleep and Guan Yu go to die heroicaly in battle. I was a bit surprised at Guan Yu having diety status to start with. But I liked that he kept coming back as a ghost and saved his son on more than one occasion. Zhuge Liang went to fight the Nan Man. Which I enjoyed immensely as that was by far the hardest level in the video game I have so I really wanted to see what his master strategy against the elephants was! <br /><br />I'm still enjoying it, though of the "four classic novels" that I've read I think this is the 3rd best. But it's good to read. Only 500 or so more pages to go!<br /> <br />I'm finally finished with Three Kingdoms! I have to say I was more than underwhelmed with the last part. It took a severe effort of will to bother finishing it at all. All the cool deaths happened in volume 3, 4 just seemed to mostly be about people I didn't really care that much about. I was looking forward to reading "the ruse of the empty city", but found it to be only a very short description in the chapter which was quite disappointing. I guess Zhuge Liang's death was a little interesting but at that point I had grown kinda bored with him, despite his great strategies against Sima Yi all he seemed to managed to accomplish was a successful retreat every time. Not very impressive. On a brighter note though I did play Sima Yi tonight in "Dynasty Warriors 4" and that was lots of fun he got to shoot purple that matched my hair!<br /><br />Hmmm, I seem to be incapable of a serious review, I blame the stress of moving. I did enjoy the series as a whole, I think the first half is by far the best. I loved the story of Lu Bu, the Yellow Turbans, Dong Zhou, and Cao Cao, and the early battles with Zhuge Liang plotting with the South were fun. Though I definitely felt like it lost some steam after everyone died. But I am glad I read it. My next excursion into Chinese literature in translation will be Creation of the Gods which I'm looking forward to a lot. But I should probably read the bibliogoth reading list book first.<br /> <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1266783686803803785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2753624167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2753624167" />
    <a title="Taro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3838350-taro"><img alt="Taro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1276106531p2/3838350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2753624167?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taro" href="/user/show/3838350-taro">Taro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241332771" href="/book/show/38213087-the-romance-of-the-three-kingdoms">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=pseudo-non-fiction">pseudo-non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=vengeance">vengeance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=chinese">chinese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=whatthehellisgoingon">whatthehellisgoingon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3838350-taro?shelf=public-domain">public-domain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2753624167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7503106949387336147">More like <i>Bro</i>mance of the Three Kingdoms amirite?!<br/>Three friends swear in a peach orchard to be BEST FRIENDS FOREVER. And they stick to it, avenging deaths and swearing allegiances with their relationship as the prime motivator. Many millions died for Xuande's OT3.<br/>Five pages of Main Characters, a map with 50 cities most of them not the ones mentioned, this was worse than reading a Roman biography getting all the names straight.<br/>The abridgement at first left something to be desired, but I realize i</span>
  <span id="freeText7503106949387336147" style="display:none">More like <i>Bro</i>mance of the Three Kingdoms amirite?!<br />Three friends swear in a peach orchard to be BEST FRIENDS FOREVER. And they stick to it, avenging deaths and swearing allegiances with their relationship as the prime motivator. Many millions died for Xuande's OT3.<br />Five pages of Main Characters, a map with 50 cities most of them not the ones mentioned, this was worse than reading a Roman biography getting all the names straight.<br />The abridgement at first left something to be desired, but I realize in 2019 in the West, the entire book would be unwieldy (it is already 615 pages in English) and many of the repetitive sections would get boring. So it is ok.<br />The characters turn coat more than they change their underwear. And for petty, petty reasons. "To Protect the Mandate of Heaven!" when everybody is clearly in it for their own gain.<br />Many, many innocent people died and/or conscripted into, meaningless to them wars.<br />From a story point of view, it starts out reasonably realistic, though I still can't think about the logistics of raising a trained army of 100,000 men on the fly, in the 1st century.<br />Then it slowly gets odder and odder - a ghost apparition, Daoist priest does magic, reanimated dead bodies, ghost revenges, Kong Ming has seven grains of rice in his mouth when he is buried so he can stop his star falling from heaven (tipping off the other faction), and he succeeds in pushing the star back (post-mortem), ghosts coming for revenge, and, seasoned warlords, suddenly dropping dead from surprise or shock.<br />Also gunpowder in the 1st century AD.<br />A second note to the translation, it is all in present tense. Which does link to the original Chinese being basically tenseless (compared to English grammar), but it makes it very very hard to get a sense of timing. It all feels like it is happening in the now, not over 100 years.<br /><br />I've reread my review (I generally review asap for freshness) and noticed - The story has so many subplots and biplots and time --- over 100 years and at least as many people to start. It makes me forget the scope of the opening lines:<br /><blockquote>話說天下大勢，分久必合，合久必分。 "For all things under heaven, that long apart must inevitably come together, that long together must inevitable break apart. Thus has it ever been"</blockquote>. In truth, this book hits on the transience of all things, empires most absolutely included. (Bromances excluded tho). It's quite an amazing scope.<br />Steeped deeply in Confucian filial piety, entire clans suffer - mortally - the consequences of almost infantile seeming whims. It's semi-fictional and certainly the shapeshifting Daoist priest and his zombie army is probably fiction, but most people written about here that have lived and died probably did live and die. <br />From this book, the Chinese have the expression : "Speak of Cao Cao, and Cao Cao arrives", which cognates exactly with our English "Speak of the devil"... which tells you of Cao Cao's nature. <br />Xuande's a hero mostly because half-way through (spoilers) he has the marvelous idea to <i>not</i> raise an entire city to the ground, with the people. "Don't kill any innocent people!" he orders his army. The wondrous novel idea gains him the hearts of the common people (also there were common people left to give their hearts).<br /><blockquote>玉可碎而不可改其白，竹可焚而不可毀其節。<br />Jade may be shattered, but its whiteness remains; bamboo may be burned, but its joints stand straight.</blockquote><br /><br /><blockquote><br />紛紛世事無窮盡，天數茫茫不可逃。<br />鼎足三分已成夢，後人憑弔空牢騷。<br />All down the ages rings the note of change,<br />For fate so rules it; none escapes its sway.<br />The kingdoms three have vanished as a dream,<br />The useless misery is ours to grieve.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="7503106949387336147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_130223710" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130223710" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2356657-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605306474p2/2356657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/130223710?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/2356657-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2356657-jonathan?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130223710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16748183731218190400">Really long, really good.<br/><br/>And what follows is a somewhat tedious review, so don't read it.<br/><br/>It's my first read-through and I'm not sure I can offer much in review. But my favorite characters were Zhao Zilong (for his bravery, honor, and integrity), Zhang Fei (for his heroic appetite), and Kongming (for his wisdom, but not so much for his pragmatism). Liu Bei, as portrayed, seemed to become less of the figure near the end, and this was a letdown, at least for my emotional attachment to him. Deng Ai </span>
  <span id="freeText16748183731218190400" style="display:none">Really long, really good.<br /><br />And what follows is a somewhat tedious review, so don't read it.<br /><br />It's my first read-through and I'm not sure I can offer much in review. But my favorite characters were Zhao Zilong (for his bravery, honor, and integrity), Zhang Fei (for his heroic appetite), and Kongming (for his wisdom, but not so much for his pragmatism). Liu Bei, as portrayed, seemed to become less of the figure near the end, and this was a letdown, at least for my emotional attachment to him. Deng Ai was a character one would have longed to see in greater detail. Zhou Yu was wise (and pragmatic), but somewhat conniving and jealous. 'Course, very few characters cared for more than their own interests, which made it difficult to desire to know them or in anyway emulate them.<br /><br />Wisdom and honor seemed to be conjoined twins. Though not always (Zhang Fei, Zhou Yu, and etc.). And wisdom and pragmatism are sometimes seen as equated with each other, but in some scenes pragmatism is cut off from wisdom and placed in an inferior position. The book isn't quite clear on this, I think. The book is also not clear when it renders its judgements on characters. The judgements seem very arbitrary with some characters, yet more in line with the book's themes of wisdom and honor in judging other characters.<br /><br />And unity and division, the yin and yang (which I have no knowledge of), would seem to be a major theme, if not the theme of the <u>Three Kingdoms</u>.<br /><br />I would argue that wisdom and living righteously (see honorably) are equal in importance thematically (in comparison with unity/division, at heaven's will), because even though division and unity are prominent, wisdom and honor (or the lack) are both a cause and/or buttress for, or against unity and/or division. Heaven still seems to be the ultimate judge in the events of the land, yet wisdom and honor give men place in heaven and remembrance on earth (according to the story).<br /><br />So I write all that to get my first thoughts out and down on digital paper.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16748183731218190400" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2103111539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2103111539" />
    <a title="Olga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64989966-olga"><img alt="Olga" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2103111539?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olga" href="/user/show/64989966-olga">Olga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64989966-olga?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2103111539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer417779116064139937">An exelent source if you are looking to find out more about the culture, society, morals and history of china (the last one is greatly helped by the essays in the afterword). <br/>On the other hand, I would not recomend it as a leasure read for the most people. As a novel, it gets incredibly repetitive - unless you are a hardcore military strategy fan, there is only that many times before reading about two generals fighting, one feigning defeat and leading the other one into a trap gets old. To be fa</span>
  <span id="freeText417779116064139937" style="display:none">An exelent source if you are looking to find out more about the culture, society, morals and history of china (the last one is greatly helped by the essays in the afterword). <br />On the other hand, I would not recomend it as a leasure read for the most people. As a novel, it gets incredibly repetitive - unless you are a hardcore military strategy fan, there is only that many times before reading about two generals fighting, one feigning defeat and leading the other one into a trap gets old. To be fair to those who do enjoy the study of war, such occurences do contain variation, but alas, i am not one of those. And if you are not either, this is going to be another problem - the breakdown of the novel into military strategy, political manipulations and everything else has a ratio of about 6 to 3.5 to 0.5. The amount of characters has beeen mentioned here before. what was not, is the amount of details - with regards to both the characters, and the events that happen. Prepare to be given a briefing on a family history, offices held and a general story of a life for anyone who appears for more than four pages. Prepare to be given a description every time an official has one opinion regarding a decision to be made, consults the advisiors who agree, consults advisors who disagree, gives replies to both and only then acts in one way or another. <br />Now, granted, this book was meant to be read and studied in quiet by the nobles versed in history, philosophy and the arts of war, who would be acquainted to a much different storytelling tradition. Not by an attention-deficient college student on a bus. For anyone choosing to read it, I strongly advise to research the background of the work and the actual history of the time period described. As much as it is counter intuitive to the general consensus of "no spoilers", I personally would have read this work very differently have i not started out with the blank slate. </span>
  <a data-text-id="417779116064139937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2341394026?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Koit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Koit" href="/user/show/5397189-koit">Koit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5397189-koit?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2341394026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13535143068012498262">This is, without a doubt, one of the finest books I have ever read. Not only is the translation readable and enjoyable, but it also carries the elan of the original narrative!<br/><br/>At this stage, I would normally cut in with a negative aspect, some character I did not like, or other unfavourable opinions. What is remarkable about the 'Three Kingdoms' is that although it is not missing this aspect in the story, these people and events are written in such a way that it is clearly not the author's fault </span>
  <span id="freeText13535143068012498262" style="display:none">This is, without a doubt, one of the finest books I have ever read. Not only is the translation readable and enjoyable, but it also carries the elan of the original narrative!<br /><br />At this stage, I would normally cut in with a negative aspect, some character I did not like, or other unfavourable opinions. What is remarkable about the 'Three Kingdoms' is that although it is not missing this aspect in the story, these people and events are written in such a way that it is clearly not the author's fault they are there but rather that he is writing down the history of what happened (if occasionally fictional). As such, this book is also a beautiful illustration of the fallacies of humankind -- how much wiser and cleverer we think we are, how easily we are deceived, and how much else goes on that we forget to include in our thoughts.<br /><br />Having this vantage point into a history that spans a hundred-years and in words which have become a cultural phenomenon is absolutely stunning! The simple rapidity with which the characters in the novel repeat their earlier mistakes should worry all of us, as should their chronic inability to learn from the failures of others -- but that's just how the saying goes, isn't it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="13535143068012498262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80458805-levie?shelf=chinese">chinese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80458805-levie?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80458805-levie?shelf=tip-top">tip-top</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80458805-levie?shelf=doozy">doozy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80458805-levie?shelf=emotionally-taxing">emotionally-taxing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80458805-levie?shelf=great-characters">great-characters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2795407969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2038177044450400808">This was ten times more epic then I anticipated it to be. Especially since this is about 90% historically accurate. The story spans generations and follows the sons and daughters of those whom you meet initially. <br/><br/>I wouldn't recommend this to everyone, it's very long, and some of the translations and names might be difficult for those with no exposure or knowledge of any of the Chinese dialects. I learned this first hand when I pushed these onto my friend's lap and he kept interchanging character</span>
  <span id="freeText2038177044450400808" style="display:none">This was ten times more epic then I anticipated it to be. Especially since this is about 90% historically accurate. The story spans generations and follows the sons and daughters of those whom you meet initially. <br /><br />I wouldn't recommend this to everyone, it's very long, and some of the translations and names might be difficult for those with no exposure or knowledge of any of the Chinese dialects. I learned this first hand when I pushed these onto my friend's lap and he kept interchanging characters with similarish names such as Liu Bei and Lu Bu. If you know who they are and what they are like you'd understand how ridiculous that is. <br /><br />People often compare this story to the Odessey, but I have to say, this was far more exciting than that. Sorry Homer, no offense. This did take me over a month to finish, due to the processing time I needed between battles and major events and because of the sheer length. 800,000 plus words. DOOZY. <br /><br />It's weird to think about how much-recorded history there is in Asia. And how much of Asian culture westerners have adopted as its own. This was of personal interest to me but it is truly fascinating and I would recommend it to anyone who loves history or has any interest in Chinese culture. It's worth the marathon read that it is. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2038177044450400808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It really puts the snooze-fest that is War and Peace to shame.</span>

<div id="review_2366089519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2366089519" />
    <a title="John Cravey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75129761-john-cravey"><img alt="John Cravey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514003805p2/75129761.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2366089519?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Cravey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Cravey" href="/user/show/75129761-john-cravey">John Cravey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/24769624-romance-of-three-kingdoms">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2366089519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4409452383337574166">Translation: Brewitt-Taylor, 120 chapters, 596186 words<br/>(Suggestions for more enjoyable reading are at the bottom of his review.)<br/><br/>I’ve yet to run across another book that has done a better job of integrating combat action, strategy, tactics and politics. In spite of its length I never got tired of reading it. It leaves an impression the further you read—as I progressed through the book I noticed that I began to think more strategically--like a chess player.<br/><br/>This is a historical novel, not an author</span>
  <span id="freeText4409452383337574166" style="display:none">Translation: Brewitt-Taylor, 120 chapters, 596186 words<br />(Suggestions for more enjoyable reading are at the bottom of his review.)<br /><br />I’ve yet to run across another book that has done a better job of integrating combat action, strategy, tactics and politics. In spite of its length I never got tired of reading it. It leaves an impression the further you read—as I progressed through the book I noticed that I began to think more strategically--like a chess player.<br /><br />This is a historical novel, not an authoritative history text. The history text of this period was <i>Records of the Three Kingdoms</i> and like many works of fiction based on history, many parts of the <i>Romance</i> can only have been speculation. Also, skeptics might be unimpressed by the accounts of the supernatural (visions, omens, prophetic dreams (some of them horrible nightmares), miracles, ghosts, etc.)<br /><br />But the <i>Romance</i> stands on its own as a work of fiction. And not only do the supernatural events add a dash of spice, they’re also important in illuminating what’s going on underneath the worldly events. For example, the fantastic account of Daoist magician, Zuo Ci, is probably as close to a peyote vision as most of us will ever get. But it also presents us the enigma of powerful men. Cao Cao was a great military leader and political ruler, but Zuo Ci offers him a chance to become a god. You would think that godhood would be irresistable, but Cao Cao can’t give up his earthly power and his yearning to defeat and dominate other mortals. In spite of that, powerful men are also hungry for supernatural powers. They need to see the future and science only takes them so far. They also have to dabble in religious thinking because everyone down to the humblest peasant takes the mandate of heaven very seriously. It’s so serious that it starts a civil war in China, possibly the hardest country in the ancient world in which to wage a civil war due to its massive geographic size.<br /><br />This is also the story of effective military leaders and advanced military technology in the ancient world. There were great strategists and generals like Cao Cao, Jia Xu, Xu Huang, Cheng Yu, Guo Jia, Lu Xun, Sima Yi, Deng Ai and especially Zhuge Liang. I wish that more of the tactics that were mentioned had been fully described, but there were plenty of details. For example, The Shu army suffered a major setback because of one bad gully. Combats involved not only swordsmen but also messengers, logistics, engineers (tunnels, fortifications, moats, mounds, catapults), archers, incendiaries and riverine warfare. Zhuge Liang even invented some kind of supply railroad, disguised as livestock. Even tribesmen had metallic carriages. This is one of the best novels I’ve ever read.<br /><br />4.6 stars<br /><br />Suggestions for more enjoyable reading:<br /><br />1. The first third of the book is essentially background. The story of how the Han dynasty became three separate kingdoms will be told here. The problem for you if you’re a Westerner (like me) is that it is chock full of Chinese names. In the course of the book you’ll become familiar with Liu Bei, Guan Yu, Zhang Fei, Cao Cao, Sun Quan, Sima Yi and Zhuge Liang. You might want to be aware of great generals and strategists like Cheng Yu, Guo Jia, Jia Xu, Lu Xun and and Xu Huang. But there are hundreds more and they’re not always easy to remember. Therefore, make a “cheat sheet” with what I would consider to be the most important names along with a small bit of biographical information on each. (Try to limit this information to their rank, skill and who they served--You want to avoid including spoiler data.) Do this for the names I’ve already mention and add Chen Gong, Cheng Pu, Ding Yuan, Dong Zhuo, Fan Chou, Guo Si, He Jin, Huangfu Song, Jian Shuo, Li Jue, Li Su, Lu Bu, Lu Zhi, Ma Teng, Tao Qian, Wang Lang, Wang Yun, Xun Yu, Yuan Shao, Zhang Ji, Zhang Yang, Zhou Yu and Zhu Jun. You should be able to ignore the rest. The good news is that, after the background is covered, there won’t be many more names to remember.<br /><br />2. You’ll enjoy the book much more if you have a set of good maps. If your edition of the book lacks maps, you can find decent maps on the internet. I found two or three maps that were useful. I found the most useful map by googling “Map of the Three Kingdoms” and then downloading the map with the white land mass with the word “WEI” in blue, “SHU” in red and “WU” in green. The problem is that this map is huge. What I did was to use my graphics software to cut this map into smaller pieces. The best idea is to cut them centering on the borders of the kingdoms because that’s where the battles will be. I printed the JPG as a PDF document. Then I found an online utility that converted PDF to MOBI format and put the maps on my Kindle (or did I just put the PDF on the Kindle? Try both to see which works). It was a bit of a pain to keep leaving the book to look at the maps, but at least I had some sense of where these guys were fighting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4409452383337574166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3506530269" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3506530269" />
    <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant"><img alt="Stephen Durrant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362701086p2/1457001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3506530269?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Durrant" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant">Stephen Durrant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780520215856" href="/book/show/2099102.Three_Kingdoms">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3506530269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4010682449888993100">One of the six classic Chinese novels from the Ming-Qing period. "Three Kingdoms" is a historical novel but reflects, as the narrative progresses, the rise of its central characters from a historical to a legendary, and then, towards the end, an almost mythological status. That is, it witnesses in its very narrative a sort of apotheosis of Lord Guan, Liu Bei, Zhang Fei, and Zhu Geliang, which any visitor to various temples in the contemporary Chinese world might still see enshrined in brightly c</span>
  <span id="freeText4010682449888993100" style="display:none">One of the six classic Chinese novels from the Ming-Qing period. "Three Kingdoms" is a historical novel but reflects, as the narrative progresses, the rise of its central characters from a historical to a legendary, and then, towards the end, an almost mythological status. That is, it witnesses in its very narrative a sort of apotheosis of Lord Guan, Liu Bei, Zhang Fei, and Zhu Geliang, which any visitor to various temples in the contemporary Chinese world might still see enshrined in brightly colored and dramatically posed statuary. But most of all this is an engaging and sometimes exciting tale of loyalty, warfare, military strategy and the ever-shifting world of political alliances. Moss Robert's translation is rich and readable--in fact, it is something of a masterpiece. His full translation (2000+ pages) now awaits on my shelves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4010682449888993100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Leslie Crawley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45574314-leslie-crawley"><img alt="Leslie Crawley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461105343p2/45574314.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/29084447-three-kingdoms">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1661569431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13698729700398934960">A Classic Novel in Four (4!!!) Volumes. Big volumes. (In a nutshell) recounts the kingdoms of Wu, Wei and Shu vying to dominate China proper after the fall of the Eastern Han dynasty. <br/><br/>I do recommend this to anyone interested in:<br/>a: Historical novels<br/>b: Chinese literature<br/>c: Someone with a large period of time on their hands!<br/><br/>This is convoluted and so very intensive on the memory, but it is also so richly rewarding as well, and worth the (not insignificant) effort to read. <br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('scuTx5Gg6n1RV7B6ZX5TrcdqMn9FKM/6uvQWrl1QndajGKc1t/ZaXjK3ejB9rjoFdrDRA0cG0didhJ81ISRmGw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14946922115512378426"><b>Luo Ben</b> (c. 1330–1400), better known by his style name <b>Luo Guanzhong</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13733431.___' title='罗贯中' rel='nofollow noopener'>罗贯中</a>) (Mandarin pronunciation: [lwɔ kwantʂʊŋ]), was a Chinese writer who lived during the Yuan Dynasty. He was also known by his pseudonym <i>Huhai Sanren</i> (Chinese: 湖海散人; pinyin: Húhǎi Sǎnrén; literally "Leisure Man of Lakes and Seas"). Luo was attributed with writing <i>Romance of the Three Kingdoms</i> and editing <i>Water Margin</i>, two of th</span>
  <span id="freeText14946922115512378426" style="display:none"><b>Luo Ben</b> (c. 1330–1400), better known by his style name <b>Luo Guanzhong</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13733431.___" title="罗贯中" rel="nofollow noopener">罗贯中</a>) (Mandarin pronunciation: [lwɔ kwantʂʊŋ]), was a Chinese writer who lived during the Yuan Dynasty. He was also known by his pseudonym <i>Huhai Sanren</i> (Chinese: 湖海散人; pinyin: Húhǎi Sǎnrén; literally "Leisure Man of Lakes and Seas"). Luo was attributed with writing <i>Romance of the Three Kingdoms</i> and editing <i>Water Margin</i>, two of the Four Great Classical Novels of Chinese literature.</span>
